92.   The olirious conclusion is that the potential benefits of the A.S. far outweigh
the risks assnciated with the N.A. S., provided the required sensitivity and flexibility
are applied to its implementation. This is why special attention has been paid, in the
design of the program, to realistic phasing in of the reforms, to alternative strategies
for achieving specific objectives, and to the monitoring of their social impact.
G.    Implementation
1.   Implementation Responsibilities. Readiness and Institution Building
93.   Day-to-day implementation of the nroaram would be entrusted to MEN's concerned
Directors, forming a Comite de suivi, which would hold at least one monthly meeting on
implementation of all aspects of the project and one quarterly meeting to examine the
"Tableau de Bord' showing progress toward the key indicators in the adjustment program.
This MEN Comnittee would be assisted by the Interministerial Ccmission created under
the Chairmanship of the Minister of Finance to monitor the macro and financial impact
of ongoing and forthcoming adjustment projects in Mali. This high level Commission
would be involved only in preparing the Joint Annual Review, participating in it, and
ensuring that the Draft Budget (Loi de Finances) and three-year investment program
reflect the agreements reached under the program. Each year a joint Government - IDA
review would be held, to cover implementation of (i) the adjustment program, (ii) the
accompanying work program (studies and measures); and (iii) the investment element. All
the donors supporting the Program/Project would participate, and arrangements have been
developped to ensure close operational coordination between them. At negotiations,
assurances were received from the Government that beginning in 1989, the review would
be held no later than November 30 of each project year (i.e., when statistics for the
previous school year are known and before the budget law is finalized).
94.   For each component of the investment element, implementation responsibility would
rest with the relevant MEN Directorate, while the PCU would act as liaison with IDA for
all matters related to project execution and providing support in specific areas as
indicated in para.  83.   All preparatory activities -- implementation plans and
schedules, survey of construction needs, designs and guides for the construction
program, requirements for goods -- have been taken to an advanced stage of preparation
and start-up activities have begun under a Project Preparation Facility. Draft bid
documents are being prepared with the assistance of ILO's Equipro and would be available
by credit effectiveness. Although previous experience with the sector has been good,
implementation has been cautiously planned over a six-year period to take into account
the greater complexity of this project. The implementation arrangements and schedules
agreed to at appraisal are summarized in Annex XI and detailed in Annex 3-5 of the I.B.
95.   The proposed project would provide a total of 137 man-years of specialist
services (of which 65 percent by local staff), and a total of 454 man-years of training
(of which 94 percent locally arranged) comprising 35 man-years for initial training, 370
man-years for in-service training, 49 man-years for management training. Priorities,
criteria and arrangements for staffing and training were agreed at appraisal. Terms of
reference for the specialist services and training were agreed upon at negotiations.
Conditions of disbursement of the IDA credit would be: 8)  for the construction
component (paras. 61-66), signing of the FAC-financed contract for the volunteers; and
(ii) for the management component (paras. 78-83) signing of the FAC-financed twinning
contract with the Institute for Research on Educational Economics mentioned in para. 76.
Details are given in Annex XI. Tables 4 and 5.



- 35 -
2.   Procurement, Disbursements and Special Account
96.    Procurement  under  the  investment  element.    Procurement  arrangements  are
summarized in Annex X. Works for general contracts and goods (including furniture,
vehicles, computers, equipment, pedagogical materials and printing of textbooks)
totalling US$8.0 million equivalent, or about 35 percent of the total value of contracts
for works and goods financed by IDA, would be procured through international competitive
bidding (ICB) in accordance with IDA guidelines and grouped, to the eKtent practical,
to facilitate competition in bidding, into bid packages of at least US$100,000
equivalent.  Where ICB procedures are used, a preference margin would be given to
domestic contractors and manufacturers in accordance with IDA guidelines; this margin
would not exceed 7.5 percent for works, and 15 percent or the applicable customs duty,
whichever is less, for goods manufactured locally.
97.   Other contracts for civil works,  school furniture, office supplies and non
pedagogical equipment, estimated to cost less than US$100,000 and financed by IDA
(totalling US$3.0 million equivalent), because they:   (i) are small in size; (ii)
involve widely dispersed sites; (iii) apply labor intensive techniques; and (iv)
correspond to goods available locally at prices below the international market, would
be awarded through competitive bidding advertised locally (LCB) in accordance with
procedures acceptable to IDA. During negotiations the Halian delegation indicated MEN's
agreement to apply the following principles: (a) the aggregate amount of such
procurement would not exceed the equivalent of US$3.1 million equivalent; (b) they would
be advertised locally and bidders would be allowed sufficient period for submission of
bids (45 days minimum); (c) evaluation criteria would be clearly specified -- price,
capacity and capability to perform, adequacy of equipment and personnel, and financial
capacity; (d) all bids would be opened in public and bidders' representatives would be
allowed to be present; (e) no preference margin would be granted to domestic
contractors, suppliers and manufacturers; (f) eligible foreign firms would not be
precluded from participation and would not be required to be incorporated in Mali in
order to participate in the bidding; and (g) copy of draft standard bidding documents
would be submitted to, and found acceptable by, IDA prior to approval of the first
contract by the Association.
98.   Incremental operating costs over a six-year period and items (office supplies,
spare parts, fuel) which cannot be grouped into bid packages of at least US$10,000,
provided the aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed US$1.93 million
equivalent, would be procured through international andlor local shopping on the basis
of price quotations obtained   from at least three reliable suppliers to ensure
competitive prices, in accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA.
99.   IDA-financed "Matching Funds' would be channelled through the Primary Education
Fund (FAEP) to leverage the financial efforts of communities to construct, rehabilitate
and equip primary schools.   They would be paid to established APE committees on a
tranche basis, provided works and/or equipment have been delivered and prefinanced in
accordance with quantity and quality agreed with the FAEP. Given their number (about
160) and small size (less than US$60,000 equivalent each), contracts for works and
construction materials totalling US$9.99 million equivalent, would be procured following
established commercial practices (local shopping), provided the contract awards would
be based on a comparison of price quotations from at least three contractors. The
Project Coordinating Unit would be responsible for verifying and certifying to IDA that
this procedure is followed.



- 36 -
100.   Specialist services and training financed by IDA (totalling US$3.44 million
equivalent) would be contracted in accordance with IDA's Guidelines for the use of
consultants.
101.  About 190 bidding packages/contracts for works and goods financed by IDA are
expected to add up to US$21.14 million equivalent. With a threshold of US$100.000 for
prior Bank review of procurement documentation, about 10 percent of the total number of
contracts would cover about 45 percent of the value contracted for works and goods. The
remaining 55 percent of the value contracted would be subject to either: (i) post review
for contracts estimated at between USS1O.000 and US$100.000 equivalent; or (ii) random
review by IDA supervision missions for contracts estimated at less than US$10,000 for
which expenditures will be made out of certified statements of expenditures (SOEs),
and for which documentation would be retained at the Project Coordination Unit.
102.   Procurement under the Sectoral Adiustment Program.  The quick-disbursing element
(totalling US$20.7 million of which US$3 million would be financed by the IDA credit,
US$9.0 million under SPA and the rest by Government) would provide foreign exchange to
support eligible imports for the general development of all levels of the education
sector.   It would finance a positive list of expenditures comprising:   textbooks,
pedagogical materials, science/laboratory equipment, office supplies, equipment,
furniture, computers, vehicles, spare parts and consumables.  Goods financed by IDA
would be limited to direct imports to cover the CIF price of goods and would be grouped
into bid packages of at least US$100,000; procurement would be made through ICB in
accordance with IDA guidelines. Where funds provided by other donors under SPA are not
untied, procurement of goods would be made in accordance with the procurement
requirements of other donors. Reimbursement of fuel for operational activities, would
not exceed 5 percent of the total value of the quick-disbursing element. The Borrower,
with the PCU's support, would be responsible for ensuring that    the disbursement
requests submitted concern only eligible items procured in accordance with procedures
consistent with  the  different  Guidelines  for Procurement.    If any  particular
disbursement  request were  rejected due to either  ineligibility or unacceptable
procurement procedures, the Borrower would be allowed to substitute it with other
eligible and acceptable procurement.  The Credit Agreement would modify the standard
procurement procedures  for ICB set out for the investment program by including
simplification of the bid invitation procedure -- limited to a General Procurement
notice in 'Development Business" --- and the elimination of domestic and regional
preferences.   Under the adjustment program, Government agreed, within the existing
malian procedures, to a major improvement in MEN's procurement packaging and planning
(para. 56) which would result in savings of at least 10 percent on the Ministry's
purchases of goods. The PCU and DAF would receive assistance in procurement through a
twinning arrangement with EQUIPRO (a branch of ILO). The procurement procedures and
arrangements outlined above, which are detailed in Annex X and in, the Development
Credit Agreement, will apply throughout the life of the project.
103.  Disbursements would follow two different procedures.  For the Quick-disbursing
element, they  would be made against direct imports of goods purchased by MEN; a
positive list of eligible expenditures is shown in Annex X.  The counterpart funds
generated would be used to finance general development expenditures in the sector.
Disbursements would be made in three equal tranches during the first three years of the
project (effectiveness, and following November 1990 and 1991 Joint Annual Reviews);
release of each tranche would be conditional on satisfactory progress in implementing
the policies and meeting the quantified targets of the adjustment program (detailed,
monitorable conditions are in Annex XII). The Borrower would prefinance all procurement



- 37 -
and subsequently claim reimbursement from IDA; the credit would reimburse 100 percent
of the cost of directly imported goods on the basis of evidence that they were purchased
and paid for by Mali not more than four months prior to credit signing. The minimum
application for reimbursements would normally be for the equivalent of US$500,000. For
the investment element, the regional disbursement profile is seven years. However,
because most IDA-f inanced activities are scheduled to be completed during the first five
years of the project, the IDA credit would be disbursed over a period of six years on
the basis of the categories shown in Annex X (Table 2). The physical investment element
of the project is expected to be completed by June 30, 1994. Given the availability of
the Special Account, the minimum application for direct payments would normally be for
the equivalent of US$10,000.   The amounts to be financed under each category are
presented in Annex X. The estimated quarterly disbursements for the IDA credit are
shown in Annex X, Table 3, while Table 2 summarizes national contributions to the
project.
104.  Special account.   To facilitate disbursement (excluding the quick-disbursing
element, for which withdrawals will be used for reimbursing payments already made by
Government), US$1.5 million equivalent, denominated in CFA francs, would be advanced
from the IDA credit and deposited in a Special Account opened by the Borrower in a local
commercial bank. The amount is estimated to cover about three months of expenditures.
The Special Account would be replenished by su'bmitting withdrawal applications (WAs) to
IDA. WAs would be aggregated into pack, ges of at least US$20,000 equivalent and would
be fully documented; expenses related to matching funds for the Primary Education Funds
(PAEP) and to contracts valued at less than US$10,000 equivalent, would be reimbursed
against Certified Statements of Expenditures (SOEs), for which documentation would be
retained at the Project Coordination Unit (PCU) for random review by Bank staff during
supervision missions and for regular annual audits andlor special audits.  In addition
to the normal procedure, SOEs under the 'Matching Funds' category, would be subject to
certification by an independent auditor that payments are made and works performed in
accordance with FAEF's  regulations.   Reimbursement applications would include a
statement of account movements since the last application, with the balance certified
by the bank holding the account, and a reconciliation showing that the balance
represented is the original amount less payments awaiting reimbursement of small working
advances.
3. Monitoring and Tranche Release
105.  Para. 93 describes the implementation responsibilities agreed with Government.
The joint reviews would cover implementation of the program and the investment element
including annual budgets and work programs for each component.  The release of the
second and third Tranches would be conditional upon satisfactory completion of the
Annual Reviews, based on the monitorable conditions listed in Annex XII. In the event
these conditions were not met and the tranches were not released, disbursements under
the investment element would normally not be held up. However, Government performance
in implementing its structural adjustment program, insofar as it would impact on the
proposed operation, would be taken into account in the tranche release decisions. In
order to assist MEN in organizing the Annual Reviews, two missions by the Institute
for Research on Educational Economics twinned with MEN would be scheduled each year,
respectively in July for preparation of the budget and in September for preparation of
the Annual Review documentation, which would be the responsibility of the PCU and would
involve clearance by the Interministerial Commission. The Project Director would serve
as Secretariat for the Comite de suivi and would send to IDA a quarterly interim report
on the Comit4's Review of implementation and the Tableau de Bord. The Secretariat for



- 38 -
the Interministerial Commission (the Project and DAP Directors), would prepare the
documentation on which the Annual Review would be based. At negotiations, assurances
were received thats (i) by November 30 each year starting in 1989, Government and IDA
would exchange views on Project implementation, including the sectoral adjustment
Program; and (ii) by September 30 each year, Government would send IDA a progress report
on the Project and the Program, its revised three-year rolling Program of Investments
and Public expenditures, and its draft budget for the following fiscal year.
4. Accounting, Auditing and Reporting
106.  The PCU would maintain separate accounts for the IDA credit, the line of credit,
the quick-disbursing component, the FAEF funds, the co-financing funds and Government
counterpart financing. These accounts would be further subdivided into categories of
expenditures incurred for each component.  At negotiations, Government gave assurances
thats (i) all project accounts, including disbursements against SOEs, will be audited
annually by an independent auditor acceptable to IDA; (ii) the Government will submit
the audit reports to IDA for review not later than March 31 of each year, beginning in
1990; (iii) the audit reports will certify that the funds were used for the purposes for
which they were provided; and (iv) the PCU will submit to IDA: (a) semi-annual reports
on project implementation and expenditures by June 30 and December 31 of each year,
beginning in 1989; (b) by September 30 each year, beginning in 1989, a preparatory
report for the November annual review with IDA showing progress in implementing the
program and the investment element; (c) the draft budget for education for the upcoming
fiscal year; and (d) a completion report within six months of the IDA credit closing
date. Revolving Funds for the FAEF and the line of credit for young graduates will be
subject to SpeSial Audits. These audits would be carried out on a routine basis, by an
independent auditor; the annual audit report would be concluded by a separate auditor.
H.    Benefits and Risks
107.  Benefits.  By reversing the recent decline in the primary enrollment ratio and
efficiency indicators and focusing human capital formation on the highest priorities
(primary education and quality), the proposed project would begin to alleviate a
critical constraint to the country s long-term growth prospects. The adjustment progr&m
would improve the overall efficiency of the sector, allowing a major reallocation of
resources in favor of primary education to help broaden the base of the education
pyramid and of materials to improve quality at all levels. A combination of actions on
the supply and the demand side would result, by 1994, in an increase in the total
(public and private) primary enrollment ratio from 31 to about 36 percent, and in the
female enrollment rate from 24 to about 28 percent.  Improvements in the policy and
institutional context would heighten the productivity of the proposed, and other future,
investments, facilitating the mobilization of additional resources for the sector.
108.  The positive impact on quality of more relevant programs, adequate supplies  of
textbooks, and more motivated, better trained teachers, following more active pedagogic
approaches would translate into better student achievements. The impact would be felt,
not only in primary education (with savings of 9 to 36 percent in the unit costs per
primary graduate through a reduction of repeater and drop-out rates) but, eventually in
the other levels as well. Graduates entering the labor force would be more adaptable
and more productive. Overall, the project would initiate a gradual shift toward an
education system that would be more equitable, more cost-effective and better adapted
to Mali's projected trends in fiscal revenues and job market opportunities.



- 39 -
109. Risks. The main risks concern: ti) the Government's ability to implement the
more politically sensitive elements of the sectoral adjustment program (scholarship
reductions and tighter perronnel management) if faced with strong resistance from vocal,
vested interest groups; (ii) the fact that demand constraints on the enrollment ratio
are more difficult to address than supply constraints; and (iii) MEN's capacity to
absorb change. The first risk is mitigated, first, by the commitment of Government to
the program; second, by the flexibility built into the sectoral adjustment program to
achieve specific objectives through various combinations of measures. The other two
risks would be reduced through systematic monitoring from the beneficiaries viewpoint,
careful phasing of project activities, and yearly in-depth reviews. These risks are
manageable and well worth taking, given MEN's determination to improve the linkage
between education and employment and the deepening of our policy dialogue.
PART IV. BANK GROUP OPERATIONS
110.  CountrY strategy.  IDA's operations in Mali have become increasingly policy-
oriented since 1983. However, while past lending sought to support structural change
through operations in the major sectors, the pressing nature of Mali's macroeconomic
problems has now made macroeconomic adjustment a central aim of the Bank's support in
the immediate future. As a result of Government's renewed commitment to its reform
program, the Public Enterprise Sector Adjustment Credit was approved by the Board late
FY88. Moreover, an IMP Standby and SAF program have been approved for 1988-89 and IDA
has begun discussions aimed at formulating a medium-term structural adjustment program,
which could be suppor-ed by a first SAL in FY91. The resulting adjustment framework
would be deepened by sector adjustment operations in human resources (including the
proposed project), r;riculture and infrastructure over the 1989-92 period.
111.  Past performance. As of March 31, 1989, the Association had extended 39 credits
to Mali, totalling US$555 million equivalent. Of these operations, 14 have been for
agriculture and related activities; 9 for transport; 3 for education; 2 for
telecommunications; 2 for energy; 2 for urban development; one each for small-scale
industries, water supply, technical assistance, rural water supply, and health; 2 for
power and 1 public enterprise sector adjustment credit which represents Kali's first
adjustment operation. In addition, the International Finance Corporation has made two
investments totalling US$2.3 million. Mali's disbursement ratio rose to 24 percent in
FY87 and 35 percent in FY88 which was relatively high for a country with no quick-
disbursing  adjustment  operations.    Project  implementation  has  been  generally
satisfactory, despite difficulties and delays caused by inadequate Government
counterpart financing. Annual IDA commitments to Mali rose from an average of US$27
million in FY82-83 to US$83 million in FY86, before falling to zero in FY87 as a result
of Government delays in implementing key policy reforms related to the proposed Public
Enterprise and Office du Niger operations.  Both of these operations have now been
approved by the Board, yielding FY88 commitments of US$98.3 million. Indicative IDA
commitments planned for FY89-90 amount to approximately US$83 million, of which about
US$50 million would be for adjustment operations in agriculture and human resources, and
US$33 million for the Second Power Project, which was presented to the Board in March
1989.
112.  Forthcoming proiects. Mali's long-term growth prospects ultimately depend on the
full realization of its natural and human resource potential. Highest priority will
continue to be given to the development of the productive sectors, and in particular the
rural sector which remains the most important for longer-term growth in the Malian
economy. Major emphasis will also be placed on human resource development, through the



- 40 -
proposed operation and a second health/population project, under preparation. which will
improve the delivery of basic health services, help reinforce the implementation of
family-planning programs, while building on successful approaches developed under the
on-going first health project in the areas of community participation and cost-
recovery.Future infrastructure projects, such as the Power II Project approved in March
1989, will attempt to ensure that the benefits of past investments are fully realized
and that Mali's long-term growth is not hampered by inadequate infrastructure.  To
maximize IDA's impact, these projects will, whenever possible, reflect a broad sectoral
orientation, focusing on the issues of investment strategy and institutional
development. Furthermore, and as a result of Mali'1 recent accession to SPA financing,
all projects, and particularly the sectoral adjustment projects, will place emphasis on
cofinancing in order to better coordinate the flow of resources in support of Mali's
growth efforts.
113.  Three projects have been approved in the education sector.  The First Education
Project (CR 420-MLI for US$ 5.0 million, effective in PY73) was closed in June 30, 1983;
the Second Education Project (CR 733-MLI, for US$10.0 million, effective in 1978) was
closed on June 30, 1983; and the Third Education Project (CR 1442-MLI and SFA 010-MLI,
totalling USS 17.3 million, effective in FY84) is now completed. These projects have
been concerned principally with technical and scientific, adult and basic education, and
plrnning. They have had a positive contribution in technical education (creation of
ECICA and a Division for technical education, support to agricultural and assistant
veterinary training), and in the establishment of a planning and 4 project management
capacities.  However, their global impact on the sector has been limited because:
Mi) their focus was too dispersed under each single operation; (ii) they did not seem
to form part of a long-term, sustained strategy; (iii) the technological choices were
not always adequate (overdesign of facilities, high cost of equipment, premature
introduction of pre-vocational subjects); (iv) in basic and primary education, their
support went to marginal experimental programs without objective qualitative evaluation
instead of addressing the central issues of access and basic inputs; and (v) the
institution building effort required to implement their policy content did not receive
sufficient attention.  Two areas of notable improvement are physical implementation
because of thorough preparation in the areas of procurement, equipment and construction,
and accounting and auditing performance.   On the other hand, the more qualitative
aspects tended to lag behind during the first two years of implementation:   for
instance, the decisions concerning IPEGs (para. 27 (b) were taken unilaterally;
technical assistance was not efficiently managed; and it took a long time for IDA-funded
studies on cost and financing issues to be accepted and for efficiency considerations
to be taken into account. The tone and level of the dialogue have considerably improved,
and the lessons from these projects have guided the design of the proposed project.
Apart from the political risks linked to the newness of the adjustment process in Mali,
the main difficulty in implementing the program will be to ensure that expedient
solutions, such as the proliferation of new institutions or hasty reversal to uneconomic
approaches are not preferred over a more systematic route which will require political
courage but offer the only chance of durably reversing the negative trends observed in
recent years.
PART V. COLLABORATION WITH THE IMP AND OTHER DONORS
114. IMF. IMF and Bank staff worked closely on preparation of the Medium-Term Policy
Framework Paper (PFP) and the first year program to be supported by the Fund's
Structural Adjustment Facility (SAF). DMF staff is in agreement with the proposed
Sectoral Adjustment Program which is fully consistent with, and complementary to the
overall macroeconomic framework.



- 41 -
115.  Other donors.  In education, other donors have systematically been kept informed
of IDA's policy dialogue with the Government. Because of the relatively low priority
assigned to the sector in the 1987-91 Development Plan and the lack of a coherent,
comprehensive long-term Government educational strategy, their interventions until
recently were, in most cases, limited and uncoordinated, focusing on secondary
technical, vocational training, PRT and individual GEs, all of which are more visible
and less diffuse than primary education. The main donors have traditionally been France
(secondary and higher education), the USSR and China (vocational and technical, higher
education), AfDB (co-financier of the Third Project and now reviewing higher education
options jointly with IDA). Following the Association and the cofinanciers involved in
the proposed operation, a number of other donors have now indicated a readiness to step
up their aid to the sector, including Cycle I, within a coordinated framework, as a
fully articulated, realistic program is put in place. Given IDA's lead role in the
sector, the strategy developed during project preparation should therefore have a
catalytic effect in attracting additional finance for the development of education in
Mali. A forum for donor coordination is providea by the UNDP-sponsored 'Groupe de suivi
EducationlFormation', and a Round Table is tentatively scheduled for 1990.
PART VI. RECOMMENDATION
116.  I am satisfied that the proposed IDA credit would comply with the Articles of
Agreement of the Association and recommend that the Executive Directors approve the
proposed Credit.
Barber B. Conable
President
Washington, D.C.                           by Moeen A. Qureshi
May 17, 1989

Obiet: Lettre de Politique Educative
Monsieur le Pr4sident,
La presente lettre de Politique Educative indique LSe intentions
du Gouvernemnt du Mali pour la mise on osuvre d'un programm d'ajustement

d6finie pour co iecteur. L'objectif du programm est de rentabiliser les

comprend un programe d'ajustement sectoriel et uf projet de typo
classique. Le programs Etablit le cadre de politique sectorielle et le

4leveo desdits investissoments.  La consolidatlt%  du systbmo educatif

objectifs suivantss

l'enseipiment dispensE & tous lee niveauz; et

financiIres et matErielles de l'ensemble du secteur.
Les difficultes roncontrEes ces dernires aundes dont la

REPUBLIC OF MALI
EDUCATION SECTOR CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Investment Element
1.          Investment Program.  Table 1 shows the breakdown proposed for the
national investment budget during the life of the project. These proposals are
based on the assumption agreed with Government at the 1986 Public Expenditure
Review, that about 6.52 of the total Investment Budget would be allocated to
Education. The resulting amount of CFAF 5 billion p.a. represents a 150 percent
increase over MEN's actual investment in 1987-89, which suggests the need for
a major resource mobilization effort. The investment requirements for primary
education were projected during project preparation and for the other levels,
were allocated on the basis of enrollments and stated priorities. The cost of
projects in the pipeline was then compared to the total investment proposed.
The results showed that: (i) 'firm projects represent about 352 of proposed
investment for the next eight years; (ii) the proposed operation accounts for
75? of all firm investments in the pipe-line; (iii) by level of education.
commitments represent 46Z of the allocation for Cycle I, 0? for Cycle II, 7S for
secondary geenral, 342 for higher education and 73t for the other levels. The
1989-91 investment program was agreed at negotiations.

ANNEX V
- 63 -                 Page 2 of 4
2.          Investment element of the proposed project.  In order to guide the
Ministry of Education in the implementation of this project, which is more
complex than what they have been used to, a detailed Implementation Book (I.B.)
was prepared.   For each element of the project, especially the investment
element, the I.B. provides background analysis, objectives, strategy, expected
outcomes, administration responsibilities, implementation schedules, means and
cost. The I.B. is available on request. In addition, the following sections
provide detailed information on what each of the investment components of the
proposed project would finance.
I.    Increasing the SupplY of. and Demand for. Primary Education
3.          Increasing the suppll of primary education in urban areas. (Para.
63 of the President's Report).   The vroject would finance:   (i) matching
contributions for the rehabilitation, repair and construction of about 1,500
classrooms; (ii) civil works contracts for the improvement of about 375
classrooms for double-shift; (iii) school furniture; (iv) a kit of basic
classroom supplies for the new classrooms; (v) 5.5 man-months to establish
guidelines for school construction and rehabilitation and to assist DET in
program monitoring and evaluation; (vi) 144 man-months of volunteers, 180 man-
months of local construction specialists (regional coordinator), and 180 man-
months of community organizers; (vii) three regional workshops for start-up and
village-level sensitization; (viii) equipment and vehicles; (ix) housing for
national specialists and volunteers; (x) overtime compensation equivalent to 12.5
percent of average teacher salaries, to be paid for 9 months p.a. (the duration
of the school year) to teachers selected for the double-shift experiment, up to
a maximum of 375 teachers in PY 5; and (xi) operating costs. Total: US$9.4
million.
4.          Stimulating social demand in the rural areas and providing incentives
to the development of private educational services. (Paras. 64 and 65). The
proiect would finance: (i) FAEF funds (US$0.8 million) to match contributions
from rural APEs for self-help construction, groups of IPEG graduates (district
schools), and village associations (village schools); (ii) about US$0.5 million
for promotional activities and qualitative improvements at the school level;
(iii) about 3 man-months of local and foreign specialist services to analyze
specific issues and design special programs. Totals US$ 2.1 million.
5.          Promoting female participation.  (Para. 66).   The proiect would
finance:    (i)  minor  renovations  for either  rehabilitating  the  ENTF  or
transforming one of the regional IPEGs (Sikasso or Segou) into a female
vocational center; (ii) furniture and equipment for this Center; and (iii) 6
man-months of specialist services for the necessary (pre-investment and gender
issues) studies. Total US$0.1 million.
II.   Improvinx the Performance and Relevance of Primary Education
6.          Qualitative monitoring and evaluation.   (Para. 69).  The proiect
would finance: (i) 24 man-months of training abroad in the area of achievement
tests adaptation, administration and analysis; (ii) 10 man-months of short term
specialist services in the same disciplines; (iii) seminars to initially
sensitize the staff of inspectorates and schools included in the sample on the
importance and use aethodology for quality control, and subsequently, (one
seminar every other year for each of the three) to analyze and interpret the
results; and (iv) selected incremental operating costs. Total: US$ 0.4 million.



ANNEX V
- 64 -                  Page 3 of 4
7.          Teacher pre- and in-service training.  (Paras. 70 and 71).  The
proiect would finance: (i) civil works, light printing and library equipment
to transform the existing Bamako IPEG into a Center for continuous upgrading;
(ii) 24 man-months of training abroad in teacher training program development,
management and evaluation; (iii) about 12 man-months of specialists in pedagogy,
psychology, training to assist with revision of the IPEG program and to develop
the pedagogic advisor intensive course; and (iv) the cost of per diem,
transportation and documentation required for completing the 1987-88 in-service
program, interrupted for lack of resources, and for carrying out the three-tiered
in-service training program (all inspectors and pedagogic advisors, 1,000 school
principals, 3,000 teachers). Total: US$1.3 million.
8.          Primary curriculum adaptation.  (Para. 72).    The proiect would
finance: (i) 24 man-months of training abroad in applied pedagogy, curriculum
development and evaluation; (ii) 27 man-months of international specialists to
assist the task force on curriculum adaptation (including evaluation of the
ruralization and national language experiment); (iii) the operating costs of the
Task Force, including sensitization seminars for pilot teachers, a workshop on
distance teaching the printing of experimental material, pilot schools field
supervision, teacher training. Total: US$0.5 million.
9.          Pedagogic materials.   (Para. 73).   The proiect would finance:
(i) renovation of storage facilities at the central levels; (ii) printing or
purchase of about 1,100,000 textbooks in French and Arabic, as well as about
40,000 teachers'guides, about 1,300 sets or basic documentation and 7,300
classroom kits; (iii) furniture and equipment for IPN; (iv) 51 man-months of
training abroad in textbook development, adaptation, publishing, purchase and
evaluation techniques; and (v) 9 man-months of specialist services in publishing,
procurement, and printing equipment maintenance. Total: US$ 4.1 million.
10.         Pedagotic support and control.   (Para. 74).   The vroiect would
finance: (i) yearly seminars on decentralized management for all inspectors and
pedagogic advisors; (ii) minor furniture and equipment for the 31 inspectorates;
(iii) 31 small 4x4 vehicles; (iv) 3 man-months of specialist services to provide
organization and programming assistance; (v) travel and per diem for local
training; and (vi) selected incremental operating costs. Total: US$ 1.6 million.
III.  Develovint and Initiating a Restructuring Strateey for Secondary and Hither
Education (Paras. 75-77)
11.         The project would finance:  (i) civil works for the renovation of
existing facilities for the Higher Education Directorate and for the EHEP, ENI
and ENSUP libraries; (ii) furniture and specialized office equipment for the two
Directorates (including three microcomputers, of which one for the scholarship
unit) and for the Libraries; (iii) a total of six man-months of study tours for
the staff of DESGT and DESRS; (iv) 30 man-months (24 foreign, 6 local) of
training in the management of technical and higher education systems, and 10 man-
months of overseas librarian training; (v) 18 man-months of specialists services
for studies on secondary higher education, for developing and operating a
computerized scholarship system, and 36 man-months of SME financinglbanking
technique specialist; 24 man-months of local specialist services to assist with
preparation of sub-project proposals for funding under the line of credit, and
for auditing services; (vi) US$0.5 million for the line of credit; and (vii)
selected related incremental operating costs. Total: US$ 3.5 million.



ANNEX V
- 65 -                  Page 4 of 4
IV.   Strengthenint MEN's Plannina and Management Capacity
12.         Planning.  (Para. 79).   The Proiect would finances   (i) one 4x4
vehicle for DNPES;  ($i) 30 man-months of short-term specialist services in
educational planning and education economics; 10 man-months for a study on the
labor market project and demand; 24 man-months of long computer specialist to
assist computerization of DNPES and the other Directorates; (iv) about 72 man-
months of training abroad in educational planning, economics, statistics and
school mapping for the staff of the DREs and the inspectorates; (vi) about 10
man-months of study visits abroad; and (vi) selected operating costs.  Total:
US$0.8 million.
13.         Financial and personnel management.  (Para. 80). The proiect would
finance: (i) civil works to relocate DAF in renovated facilities, furniture and
equipment (including three microcomputers); (ii) 20 man-months of specialists
services in computerized budgetary procedures, cost and expenditure control
systems, procurement , personnel management, training program design, and 12 man-
months for the personnel study; (iii) 16 man-months of training abroad in
education budgeting/costs/financing, school systems administration/management,
procurement and 70 man-months of local training in the same disciplines and in
computer management techniques; and (iv) selected incremental operating costs.
Total: US$0.7 million.
14.         Decentralized management of basic education. (Para. 81). The nroiect
would finance: (i) a total of 11 4x4 vehicles, office equipment and furniture
(including micro computers), for DNEF and one for each of the eight DREs; (ii) a
total of 3 man-months of short-term specialist services to help design and
initiate the training program for DREs and IEFs; (iii) the costs associated with
the yearly local seminar in education sector administration and management,
educational planning and financing for DRE staff and IEF; and (iv) selected
operating costs. Total: US$0.6 million.
15.         Physical resource management.   (Para. 82).   The proiect would
finance: Mi) extension and renovation of existing facilities for the DET; (ii)
2 vehicles and 6 motorcycles, furniture and equipment for the DET; (iii) 12 man-
months of training abroad for DET's chief, procurement specialist and section
chiefs, as well as the costs of local project launch seminars; (iv) 36 man-months
of a school construction/maintenance specialist and 8.5 man-months of specialist
services for the preparation of school construction/maintenance guides and system
project (both provided by the twin institution); (v) 180 man-months of locally
recruited architect and draftsman services; (vi) 2 man-months of observatory
visits in neighboring countries; (vii) 180 man-months of volunteer services;
(viii) the cost of producing and disseminating the guidelines on construction
/maintenance techniques, and the cost of seminars on physical resource management
at IPEGs and DREs; and (ix) selected operating costs. Total: US$1.2 million.
16.         Proiect coordination.   (Para. 83).   The proiect would finance:
(i) the renovation and extension of the PCU facilities to make it more
functional; (ii) office furniture and equipment, including microcomputers;
(iii) 96 man-months of long-term specialists for the administrator (5 years) and
the accountant (3 years); (iv) salaries for support staff (financed by the
Government); (v) 63 man-months of short-term specialist services in accountinp,
auditing, preparation of annual review and future project preparation; (vi) '
man-months of study tours to visit other project units and attend professional
seminars; and (vii) selected incremental operating costs. Total: US$2.3 million.

REPUBLIC OF MALI
EDUCATION SECTOR CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Education Budiet. Unit Costs. and Financing
1.         The tables below are extracted from the detailed statistical Annex
2-3 of the I.B. and illustrate the analysis presented in paras. 27-31 of this
report. More specifically, Table 1 shows that by level of education, the share
of MEN's recurrent budget allocated to primary education (Cycle 1), although
growing in recent years, remains too low, both by comparison with other Sub-
saharan countries (10 percentage points), and in absolute terms because of the
high level of cost recovery In Cycle I education.  The share for Cycle II,
secondary education, and teacher training, on the contrary, is about 10 points
above the regional average; that for higher education, at 192, is similar,
although considered too high, given graduate unemployment and the higher priority
that should be assigned to expanding primary enrollments.

2.         BY types of expenditures (table 2), the structure of the budget is
substantially the same as on the rest of the continent: personnel and
scholarships, despite the latter's decline in recent years, absorb almost all

Page 2 of 11
of HEN's budget, resulting in inadequate (and declining) allocations for
materials, especially at the primary and higher levels. Mali, in the past years,
has elected to recruit more teachers at very low salaries, rather than providing
them with sufficient materials. This partly explains the deteriorating quality
and performance indicators.

4.               Regarding  the  private  financint of  education,  Table  4  shows  the
higher revenues collected from parents, whenever APE contributions are directly
related to a well-identified project ( e.g. school- based fund-raising for
improvements at the school level, as was the case hitherto in the urban areas).
By contrast, they tend to be limited to a minimum when collected through coercive



- 69 -
ANNEX VI
Page 4 of 11
methods and for ill-defined purposes, e.g. through the APE tax levied until
recently in the rural areas or the forthcoming Development tax. This explainss
(i) the hypotheses made at appraisal as to the high potential for voluntary urban
community matching contributions under the projectt and (ii) now that the GAO
decisions concerning the establishment of the Development Tax are about to be
extended to urban areas, the need to ensure a higher level of contributions by
mandating the earmarking of an adequate proportion (at least 302) of the revenues
from the Development Tax to education activities so as to maintain the level of
primary education investment in the sector.

5.                Table 5 on Unit costs illustrates para. 33 of the Report.  It shows
the rising and exceptionally high unit costs in secondary general education and
teacher training, and the comparativelv low costs in higher education. A look
at the more detailed Tables 6 and 7, respectively on unit costs in secondary and



- 70 -
ANNE VI
Page 5 of 11
higher education, indicates the need for greater transparencys for instance,
materials  allocations  in  secondary  education  are  still  budgeted  ac
'scholarships'; the share of personnel in higher education is not excessive, but
overtime represents half of it and is not entirely delivered. Unit costs in the
larger secondary schools are lower, suggesting economies of scale, but they are,
surprisingly, as high for private as for public sector schools. All of these
issues, which are discussed in detail in I.B. Annex 3-5, but require detailed
further analysis, will be addressed in the strategy for restructuring secondary
and higher education.

7.         In light of (i) the need to free up resources to expand primary
education and restore excellence in the GEs. and (ii) the oversupply of graduates
documented in Annex 8. Table 3, the following criteria for scholarship reductions
are proposed:
(a)  with respect to scholarships abroad, negotiate with host countries,
vithin a given budgetary envelope, an increase in the level of
individual scholarships so as to reduce or even eliminate the costs
presently borne by the malian budget (travel and 'trousseau'
averaging US$1,250 per student against US$5,000 borne by the host
country), in exchange for a corresponding decrease in the number of
students abroad; this could result in a saving of up to 1.3 billion
CFAP; should this approach fail, the number of new scholarships
should be limited to about 200 p.a. (down from an average of 417
during the past six years) concentrated in the countries which entail
the lowest unit costs for the Maliau Government (Table 9);
alternately, a combination of the two systems yielding about CFAP
100-120 million of savings in each of the first three years would
be proposed;
(b)  with respect to scholarships in Mali, the level of individual
scholarships should be maintained, but they should be offered only
to about half the BAC graduates; the award and renewal criteria could
be meritocratic --the first-ranking 500 graduates, split as 300 for
scientific streams and 200 for lVterary streams to compensate for
the greater difficulty of the fcrmer, suspension for second time
repeaters -- ; they could acccunt for economic differences --
awarding half the amount of the scholarship for students living in
Bamako; they could favor disciplines which are in short supply in
Mali, e.g. quotas in favor of engineering or management schools; or
they could be limited to third and fourth year students.

8.          Einancial and Enrollment implications of the nrotram.   Table 11
illustrates the impact of the budgetary and efficiency measures included in the
program on the primary education budget, primary enrollments, the number of Cycle
I graduates and Primary teacher requirements.   The assumptions used in the
modelling exercise are detailed in Annex 2-7 of the Implementation Book. The
budgetary reallocations and efficiency measures could increase the primary
enrollment ratio to as much as about 40 percent. However, the target retained
for the proposed operation - a 36 percent total primary enrollment ratio - has
been adjusted downward to reflect two factors: (i) physical implementation
capacity constraints, and the fact that the project's institution - building
effort would be focused or three of Mali's seven regions; and (ii) the fact that
the decline in the primary enrollment ratio is mostly due to a demand problem,
which is more difficult to address than supply constraints.

2.            Table  2 gives  the  flow of graduates out of basic, secondary,  and

rates to increase, which has somewhat compensated for the sharply declining
student numbers. In higher education, by contrast, the number of graduates
rose markedly in 1987, reflecting the peak intake of the early eighties.

3.        Table 3 illustrates the impact of the simultaneous increase in
the number of graduates and the decline in public/para-public sector
recruitment. ILO estimates (July 1988) that there are about 8.000-10,000
unemployed graduates countrywide; of this total, about 2,600 are graduates
from higher education, and the rest from secondary education. As shown by
the Table, about 7,400 are concentrated in Bamako. Their frustration
juxtaposed with the demotivation of teachers and students when salary or
scholarship payments are delayed (2-3 months on average in recent years) is
a serious concern for the Government.

4.        Table 4 shove the private and social rates of return on
education. The private rates are systematically higher than the social
rates because of the strong degree of public financing; this is especially
true at the post-bac level, where the scholarship policy reduces the
opportunity cost of students particularly in a low-employment situation.
Only at the primary level are both rates close, reflecting an appropriate
level of public subsidization. By level of education, both social and
private rates are high for post-bac graduates, lowest for basic education
graduates, and highest for primary (Cycle I) graduates, indicating that:
(i) continuation beyond primary education is economically justified only
for those completing higher education; and (ii) there is a need for
rigorous selection of students beyond that level. Again, primary education
is the only level which shows attractive rates even with repeaters and
unemployment. Unemployment has a much more negative impact than repeater
rates (because of the highly unequitable income structure). With two years
of post-graduation unemployment, all social rates of return are halved,
hence the recoumendation not to orient students towards streams leading to
a high probability of unemployment. When repeater rates are taken into



- 80 -
ANNEX VII
Page 4 of 5
account, the decline in the rate of return remains acceptable, except for
the Cycle II level. When repeater rates and unemployment are combined,
both social and private rates of return fall below 102, except for primary
education. Although it should be treated with caution, this type of
analysis nna Ibguously justifies the emphasis on primary education
underlying the proposed operation. Details are in Annex 2-7 of the
Implementation Book.

5.        While repeater rates do not affect markedly the economic rates
of return on education, their impact on the unit cost per araduate can be
substantial.  Table 5 below sut-aries a sensitivity analysis of the unit
cost per basic education graduate to the level of quality inputs envisaged
under the proposed operation. The detailed analysis is available In the
Implementation Book (Annex 3-4). The results indicate that with a ICFA
2,000 per student and per annum pedagogic materials injection, (equivalent
respectively to about 11 percent and 3 percent of Cycle I and Cycle II unit
costs per student), the cost per graduate could decrease between 9 and 36
percent for Cycle I and 11 and 412 for Cycle II, depending on the repeater
rate and graduation rates achieved. These savings are based on the
hypotheses that teachers would be redeployed.

Education personnel
1.   MEN personnel.  The Implementation Book contains detailed statistical
tables about MEN personnel level and structure (Annex 2-3). which are
summarized in Table 1 below. This information should be treated with caution,
because of gaps and discrepancies between various internal sources.  MEN
personnel includes about 900 MPC and 460 MSC "stagiaires n, bringing the
total number of employees to about 18,000. For the levels other than primary
education, the most striking characterictic of the system is overstaffing,
which is due mostly to the decline in student numbers in recent years . Table
2 shows the resulting decrease in the students/teacher ratio, which are very
low by world standards.
Table 1. Education Personnel
(1986-87)

3.   Teacher Salaries.  Table 4 illustrates the findings of a recent IDA
cross-country study of primary teachers in Sub-Sahara Africa. It shows that
in Mali, overstaffing is compensated by a low level of salaries. Although
as a multiple of p.c. GDP, teacher average salaries (CFAF 44,000) are
relatively high (12.6 times, against a continental range of 1.5 in Equatorial
Guinea to 14.7 in Ethiopia), this is because Mali, even within the Sahel
zone, has one of the lowest p.c. GDPs.   of all the countries surveyed
(samples of up to 25 countries), Mali is the one where: (i) the range from
the entering salary in the lower category to the maximum salary in the higher
category is the flattest -- from 1 to  1.71 --; (ii) the number of years it
has taken on average for a teacher to get from the minimum to the maximum
salary level exceeds by far that for the other countries (29 years for MPCs,
against averages of 6 to 25 years for the corresponding category elsewhere);
(iii) the yearly percentage salary change has been by far the lowest (1.47
percent for MPCs); (iv) supplements to teacher salaries, in the form of
housing, family and hardship allowances are high (equivalent to 22 percent
of teacher average salaries) but substantially lower than those in
neighboring countries (45 percent in Burkina, where salary levels are
comparable, in monetary terms, or 32 percent in Senegal, were salaries are
3.3 times higher than in Mali); (v) average teacher salaries are lower than
those of both automechanics and typists (out of an ILO sample of 9
countries). Overall, teacher salaries have fallen by lOZ in real terms since
the beginning of the decade; like all civil service salaries, they will be
frozen until 1990 under the IMF program.
4.   This  analysis  shows  that  (i)  reducing  teacher  compensation  for
controlling unit costs is not a desirable option in the Malian context, where
teacher quality and motivation will be critical to reverse the recent decline
in enrollments; (ii), the solution should, on the contrary, be in the
direction of improving incentives (salary and non-salary) to attract and
retain capable teachers while increasing the demands on teachers (increased
weekly contact hours through double-shift, or increased number of students
through multigrade, greater accountability, performance related rewards);
(iv), precise recommendations in this area can only be made on the basis of
a comprehensive study of personnel policy, recruitment and promotion,
staffing   ratios,   qualifications,   compensation,   workload,   career
opportunities) for all levels of the sector and 4n the context of a broader
administrative reform. Such a study would be submitted and discussed with
IDA by November 1990 (condition of second tranche release) and its
recommendations would be implemented according to the schedule agreed upon
at the time.

S.  Teacher training caoacity. Since the auspension, In 1983, of automatic
recruitment of IPEO graduates Into the civil service, the supply of primary
teschers has been well in excess of demand. To *djust to that situation , In
1985 admission into the 1PEG was made subject to a competitive exam and
Intake was drastically reduced. As a result, total enrollments have fallen
by about 802 In the 1PEG (1982-1987). In the ENSECa, they have fallen by 431
(1983-88).  This leaves ample unused capacity for other uses, mostly ln-
service training, until renewed growth makoes It necessary to increase again
the output of teachers.Under an on-going Utilization Plan for IPEGs, and a
forthcoming one for ENSECs (to be agreed upon at the 1989 Annual Roview), a
merging and restructuring process would be pursued under the proposed
operation. Table 5 Illustrates the output and recruitment of teachers In
1985-87.

6.   Demand for Primarv teachers. Table 6 projects new teacher requirements
under six different hypothesis, with a 2 percent wastage and drop-out rate.

would be below the projected school population growth rate,and would
therefore result In a further decline of the primary enrollment ratio; (ii)
A 5.5 percent rate, which would result in a 34 percent enrollment ratio by
1994; and (III) a 9.0 percent rate, which ould lead to a 40 percent
enrollment ratio by 1994.

7.   Balance between supplv and demand.  Table 7 shows that:
(i)  in terms of teachers availabilit,, the large existing stock of
unemployed IPEG graduates and of underutilized staff at all levels
of the system and the capacity of the 2 IPEGs kept operating
(total: 720) would go a long way towards meeting the new teachers
requirements (totality of the needs under the 5.5 percent
enrollment growth assumption, or over 314 under the 9.0 percent
growth  assumption).    Additional  needs  could be met  through
providing accelerated training to higher education unemployed
graduates (7,500 in Bamako alone), possibly employing them under
a scheme that would substitute community service (fer two years)
for military service; these options would be reviewed with
Government as the need arises;
(ii) in terms of teachers recruitment ceilings, as for teachers
availability, all possible efficiency measures (redeployment of
underutilized staff or teachers from other levels except for maths
and science teachers, quasi-freeze in recruitment at the Cycle II
and secondary levels, multigrade, double-shift) should be
exhausted, and every effort should be made to stimulate the
development of private education before a renegociation of the
civil service recruitment ceiling under the IMF program could be
justified.

REPUBLIC OF MALI
rDUCATION SECTOR CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Proiect Costs, Recurrent Cost Implications and Capital Cost Savings
1.        Basis of Costs Estimates.  Project coste are based on July 1988
prices at appraisal. Cost estimates for primary school
construction/rehabilitation are based on a Government survey of the quality
of existing classrooms and unit prices reconstructed from prices obtained
for each individual construction component; cost estimates for school
construction program are based on unit prices applied to quantities of a
low-cost prototype using local materials and labor. Other cost estimates
for works and goods are based on recent tenders in Mali or in the region
for similar types of construction and goods completed recently, with
adjustment, where necessary, for regional cost differences. The average
cost per month of internationally recruited services (US$11,000-US$12,000),
including salary, overhead and subsistence, locally recruited specialists
and consultants (US$2,250). scholarships abroad (US$2,000), and
internationally recruited volunteer specialists (US$1,700) are financed by
UNDP and other United Nations sources.
2.        Foreign Exchange Component. The estimated foreign exchange
component of the project is US$35.5 million (64Z of the total project
cost), and is the result of the following estimated foreign exchange
percentage for the individual categories of expenditurest
Civil works (general contracts)                        60S
Civil works (school construction contracts)            5OS

3.        Customs, Duties and Taxes.  Cost estimates do not include duties
or taxes. During negotiations, the Government has confirmed that the
project would be exempt from taxes and duties.
4.        Contingencies Allowances.  Cost estimates include physical
contingencies of s (i) lOZ for hardware components such as civil works,
furniture and equipment; and (ii) 52 for specialist services, training and
operating costs. Allowances for annual price escalations are as follows:

6.        Recurrent Exgenditures and Cost Savinas Generated by the Operation.
Tables 2 and 3 umsarise estimates of the cost savings and incremental recurrent
expenditures generated by the project. The detailed analysis Is in Annex 3-7 of
the Implementation Book. It should be noted that this exercise probably reflects
accurately the likely expenditure ipact of the prcject on the Government's
recurrent budget, but tends to underestimate its cost-savings effects. This
underestimate is due to a number of reasons. First, some significant benefits
have not been included beeause they are less easily quantifiable; this is the case
for the expected Increase in APE and private sector educational activit'es due to
project's incentives (FAEP), in teacher motivation due to regular salary payments,
in student achievement due to improved study conditions (pedagogic materials,
teacher in-service training, curriculum revision: nd for the more cost-effective
use of sectoral resources. Also, these Improvements will have most impact after
the project period (1994). Second, the beneficiaries will not always be the
Government; for instance, the savings resulting from the development of low-cost
primary school construction will accrue mainly to the APEs. Third, efficiency
measures (double-shift and multigrade teaching, redeployment) and the promotion oZ
private educational services will allow the enrollment of a higher number of
children for a given budget. Fourth, the reform in teacher training and the
master plan to be developed for secondary and higher education will result in
substantial savings In capital costs, as schools will be merged and streams
restructured.
7.        Disregarding the above types of benefits, the analysis shows that the
core adjustment program budgetary reallocations, savings from Improved procurement
procedures and efficiency measures would generate savings that would increase from
CFAF, 630 million In 1989 to 2,659 million in 1994 (equivalent respectively to 3.7
percent and 17.3 percent of MEN's 1988 recurrent budget); however, due mostly to
the 8.5 percent p. a projected growth (versus about 4 percent growth in previous
years) of the primary education budget and the proposed tripling of materials
allocations, the net effect would be negligible until 1992. As of 1993, unless
further budget reallocations are undertakenn, there would be net incremental costs
of 209 and 563 million CFAF, equivalent to 1.2 and 2.9 percent of MEN's 1988
recurrent budget. Sensitivity analysis have shown that these costs could be
offset by the usavings" that eould be achlved through faster introduction of
double shifts. This zero net effect is the logical consequence of the main
objective of the operation which is to divert to primary education most of the
savings realized.

REPUBLIC OF MALI
EDUCATION SECTOR CONSOLIDATIOP PROJECT
Procurement and Disbursement
I.    PROCUREMENT PROCEDURES FOR THE INVESTMENT PROGRAM
Part A.     Procurement of Goods and Works
1.          Except as provided for in Part C hereof, works and goods
(furniture, non-pedagogical equipment and pedagogical materials) shall be
procured under contracts awarded in accordance with procedures consistent with
those set forth in Sections I and II of the "Guidelines for Procurement Under
IBRD Loans and IDA Credits" published by the Bank in May 1986 (the
guidelines).
Part B.     Preference for Domestic Contractors and Manufacturers
2.          In the procurement of works and goods in accordance with the
procedures described in Part A.1 hereof, the borrower may grant a margin of
preference of 7.52 to domestic contractors and 15X to domestic manufacturers
in accordance with, and subject to the provisions of paragraph 2.55 and 2.56
of the guidelines and paragraph 5 of Appendix 2 thereof. During the appraisal
mission local authorities have been informed that procurement under local
procedures does not qualify for preference.
Part C.     Other Procurement Procedures
3.          Civil works and goods contracts estimated to cost between
US$10,000 and US$100,000 equivalent per contract, which are small in size,
involve widely dispersed sites, apply labor intensive techniques and
correspond to goods available locally at prices below the international
market, would be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of competitive
bidding advertised locally (LCB), in accordance with procedures satisfactory
to the Bank. During negotiations, the Malian delegation indicated MEN's
agreement to apply the following principlest (i) the aggregate amount of such
procurement shall not exceed the equivalent of US$3.07 million equivalent;
(ii) all bids shall be opened in public with bidder's representatives allowed
to be present; (iii) evaluation criteria shall be clearly specified and
communicated to all bidders --e.g. price performance capability, adequacy of
equipment and personnel, and financial capacity--; (iv) no preference margin
shall be granted to domestic manufacturers or contractors; (v) eligible
foreign firms shall not be precluded from participation in the bidding; and
(vi) copy of draft standard bidding documents shall be submitted to the Bank
prior to the first contract.
4.          Incremental operating costs and items (office supply, spare parts,
gasoline ...) which cannot be grouped into bid packages of at lest US$10,000



- 96 -
ANNEX X
Page 2 of 8
equivalent, provided the aggregate amount of such procurement shall not exceed
US$1.93 million equivalent, would be procured through international and/or
local shopping on the basis of quotations obtained from at least three foreign
and/or local reliable suppliers in accordance with procedures acceptable tG
the Bank.
5.          Matching Funds (totalling US$9.99 million) provided to the Primary
Edcation Fund (FAEP) acting as financial intermediary, represent subsidies to
-atch the financial efforts of communities to construcOrehabilitate and equip
primary schools. It is to the communities, with technical supports provided by
technicians from the Project coordination Unit, to undertake the procurement
with established commercial practices. Matching funds would be equivalent to
subsidies paid to established APE committees on a tranche basis, provided
work and/or equipment have been delivered and prefinanced in accordance with
quantity and quality agreed with the FAEP institution. Given the number and
the small size of contracts, local shopping would be the appropriate
commerical packages utilized by APE committees to procure small simple works,
provided the contracts awards would be made based on comparing price
quotations obtained from at least three contractors/suppliers. The Project
Coordinating Unit would be responsible for verifying aud certifying to IDA
that this procedure is followed.
Part D.     Review of Procurement Decision by the Bank
6.          Prior Review.  Between 180 and 200 bidding packages/contracts are
expected to make up the estimated US$21.14 million in the works and equipment
categories. With a threshold of US$100S000 for prior Bank review of
procurement documentation, about 1Z and 701 of the total number of the
respective packages for works and goods would cover about 51 of the value
contracted for works and about 992 for goods. On that basis between 17 and 22
contracts amounting to about US$8.6 million over the five year procurement
period would be subject to prior review, which represents a reasonable
workload for Bank staff as they would be relatively simple supply contracts.
For all contracts or bid packages of at least US$100,000 equivalent, each of
the following stages in the procurement process would be subject to the Bank's
prior review and comment upon by Bank staff before they are implemented and
finalizeds
-Scope and cost of contract;
-proposal for bidding or selection process;
-yreaualification documents (for works and goods) or preparation
of short list (for consulting services);
-advertisement (procurement notice);
-bidding documents and addenda;
-bid evaluation and proposal for award of contract'
-contract documents and subsequent major modifications;
7.          Post Review.  For all contracts or bid packages estimated at
between US$10.000 and USS100.000 equivalent, two copies of the final
contracts, together with the bidding documents, the bid evaluation report and
the award decision, would be furnished to the Bank for post review before the



- 97 -
ANNUI X
Page 3 of 8
first application for withdrawal of credit fund against these contracts. These
documents should be reviewed, as a minimum, on a sample basis to determine
that contracts have been awarded fol.lowing procedures prescribed in the credit
agreement.
8.          Random Review.  The provisions of the preceding subparagraphs of
Part D. (5) and (6) shall not apply to contracts valued at under US$l0.O0O
equivalent on account of which the Bank has authorized withdrawals from the
Credit Account on the basis of statements of expenditure. Such contracts shall
be retained at the Project Coordination Unit for random review by Bank
supervision missions and for regular ann'ial audits.
9.          Initial Review.  In order to reduce the number of formal reviews,
an initial review would accelerate the procurement process resulting in
reducing the workload for Bank staff. A copy of draft standard bidding
documents which are common to a number of operatiovs would be submitted to the
Bank for initial review before the first contract.
Part E.    S_ecialist Services and Fellowshivs or Local Trainin_
10.         Specialist services and training abroad, including audit services,
totalling US$3.44 million equivalent, would be contracted in accordance with
Bank'guideline. While the Bank recommends inviting proposals from a short list
of firms, it does not object to the borrower's requesting a proposal from a
single firm or individual specialist of its choice, provided that the Bank
approves the firm invited and, subsequently the contract. Where a number of
consultants are to be invited to submit proposals, the selection procedure and
evaluation criteria to be adopted should be determined prior to inviting
proposals and should be included in the letter of invitation. The short list
should normally comprise a wide geographic spread of firms with a lower limit
of three and a upper limit of six.
Part F.     Modification or waiver of the terms and conditions of contracts.
11.         The figure of lOZ would be specified for the purpose of paragraph
4 of Appendix 1 to the guideline. Before agreeing to any material modification
or waiver of the terms and conditions of contract which would increase the
cost of the contract by more than 10% of the original price, the borrower
shall inform the Bank of the proposed modification and the reasons therefor.

II.         PROCUREMENT PROCEDURES FOR THE QUICK-DISBURSING ELEMENT
Part G.     Positive List of Categories of Goods
12.         Purchase of equipment--at all levels of the education sector--will
consist of a positive list expenditures comprising: textbooks, pedagogical
materials, science/laboratories equipment, office supplies, equipment/
furniture, computers, vehicles, spare parts and consumables, limited to direct
imports.
Part H.     Goods Financed by the IDA Credit. (totalling US$3.0 million)
13.         The IDA Credit would finance only direct imports.  It would cover
the CIF price of goods. Goods wvuld be grouped into bid packages of at lest
US$100,000 and would be procured through ICB, in accordance with IDA
Guidelines.
14.         Where ICB is applied, the Credit Agreement will modify the
standard procurement procedures set out for the investment element by
including simplification of the bid invitation procedures and the elimination
of domestic and regional preferences. Bid invitation procedures would require
only a General Procurement Notice to be published in the United Nation's
Developm--t Business.
Part I.     Goods Financed by Other Donors Under the Special Proaram of
Assistance (SPA). (totalling USS9.0 million)
15.         Procurement of goods financed by other donors under the SPA would
be carried out in accordance with the procurement procedures agreed for donor
financed contracts.

II.       Government and Local Contributions
1.        The Government's financial contribution to the project is
estimated at CFAF 3.1 billion, equivalent to US$10.2 million or 18 percent
of the total project cost. The Government contribution represents 20
percent of the incremental operating costs and 50 percent of goods locally
procured and financed by the SPA.
2.        The contributions provided by local communities, generated from
the parents' associations (APEs) and local taxes for the school
construction/rehabilitation program are estimated at about CFAF 1.4
billion, equivalent ot US$4.5 million or 8 percent of total project cost.
In the three project areas, local communities would finance shcool
constructionlrehabilitation program; as incentives to teh further
development of private initiatives, an IDA-financed basic education fund
(FAEP) would match the financial efforts of communities, which should
represent an annual average of 50 percent (ranging between 40-60 percent)
of the school construction and rehabilitation costs.
3         The table below shows the breakdown of the Government and local
communities contributions by calendar year.
